<sup><span id="id11.11.id5.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">1</span></sup>IIIS, Tsinghua University
 <sup><span id="id12.12.id6.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">2</span></sup>Galaxea AI\
<a href="https://yuantianyuan01.github.io/FastWAM/"
class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter">https://yuantianyuan01.github.io/FastWAM/</a>\
</span></span>

</div>

<div id="abstract1" class="ltx_abstract">

###### Abstract

World Action Models (WAMs) have emerged as a promising alternative to
Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models for embodied control because they
explicitly model how visual observations may evolve under action. Most
existing WAMs follow an imagine-then-execute paradigm, incurring
substantial test-time latency from iterative video denoising, yet it
remains unclear whether explicit future imagination is actually
necessary for strong action performance.

In this paper, we ask whether WAMs need explicit future imagination at
test time, or whether their benefit comes primarily from video modeling
during training. We disentangle the role of video modeling during
training from explicit future generation during inference by proposing
<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">Fast-WAM</span>, a WAM architecture
that retains video co-training during training but skips future
prediction at test time. We further instantiate several Fast-WAM
variants to enable a controlled comparison of these two factors. Across
these variants, we find that Fast-WAM remains competitive with
imagine-then-execute variants, while removing video co-training causes a
much larger performance drop. Empirically, Fast-WAM achieves competitive
results with state-of-the-art methods both on simulation benchmarks
(LIBERO and RoboTwin) and real-world tasks, without embodied
pretraining. It runs in real time with 190 ms latency, over 4$`\times`$
faster than existing imagine-then-execute WAMs. These results suggest
that the main value of video prediction in WAMs may lie in improving
world representations during training rather than generating future
observations at test time.

</div>

<div id="S1" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">1 </span>Introduction

<div id="S1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Building general-purpose embodied agents requires policies that can not
only map visual observations to actions, but also reason about how the
physical world evolves under interaction. This has motivated growing
interest in World Action Models (WAMs), which combine future visual
prediction and action modeling in a unified framework. Compared with
standard Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models, WAMs are appealing because
modeling future observations may help capture physical dynamics and
task-relevant temporal structure.

</div>

<div id="S1.p2" class="ltx_para">

Most existing WAMs follow an imagine-then-execute paradigm: they first
generate future observations, then predict actions conditioned on the
imagined future. While intuitive, this design incurs substantial
test-time latency due to iterative video
denoising \[<a href="#bib.bib77" class="ltx_ref"
title="Mimic-video: video-action models for generalizable robot control beyond vlas">23</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib75" class="ltx_ref"
title="Video generators are robot policies">19</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="Causal world modeling for robot control">17</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib61" class="ltx_ref"
title="World action models are zero-shot policies">31</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib62" class="ltx_ref"
title="Motus: a unified latent action world model">2</a>\]. More
fundamentally, it remains unclear whether explicit future imagination is
actually necessary for strong action performance. The effectiveness of
WAMs may stem from two distinct sources: *(1)* the video prediction
objective during training, which may help the model acquire stronger
physical priors and action-conditioned representations, and *(2)*
explicit future generation during inference, which may provide
additional foresight for action prediction. Existing WAM systems
typically entangle these two factors, making it difficult to determine
which one is actually responsible for the observed gains.

</div>

<div id="S1.p3" class="ltx_para">

In this paper, we revisit this design choice and ask a simple question:
*do WAMs need to imagine future observations at test time, or do they
benefit primarily from learning to model them during training?* Our key
idea is to decouple the video prediction objective used in WAM training
from explicit future generation at inference time. If the main value of
world modeling lies in shaping better latent representations during
training, then a WAM should be able to retain this benefit without
paying the test-time cost of future video synthesis.

</div>

<figure id="S1.F1" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="2603.16666v2/x1.png" id="S1.F1.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="660"
height="366" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="S1.F1.2.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 1</span>: </span><span
id="S1.F1.3.2" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Three
representative WAM paradigms. (A) Joint-modeling WAMs denoise future
video and action tokens together. (B) Causal WAMs first generate future
observations and then condition action prediction on the generated
future representation. (C) Fast-WAM retains video co-training during
training but removes explicit future generation at inference time,
directly predicting actions from latent world representations in a
single forward pass.</span></figcaption>
</figure>

<div id="S1.p4" class="ltx_para">

Based on this perspective, we propose <span id="S1.p4.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">Fast-WAM</span>, a WAM architecture that
preserves video co-training during training but skips future prediction
at test time. Instead of using a pretrained video generation model to
iteratively synthesize future frames during inference, Fast-WAM
repurposes a pretrained video Diffusion Transformer (DiT) as a
single-pass world encoder for action generation. Concretely, we build
Fast-WAM with a Mixture-of-Transformer (MoT) architecture with shared
attention, consisting of a video DiT and an action expert DiT, as
illustrated in Figure <a href="#S1.F1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>(C). During training, the video
prediction objective shapes the video DiT to encode physically
meaningful motion and interaction structure. During inference, the video
DiT processes the observation context in a single forward pass and
provides latent world representations for action denoising, avoiding
explicit future video denoising and enabling efficient real-time
control.

</div>

<div id="S1.p5" class="ltx_para">

To study our central question in a controlled way, we instantiate
Fast-WAM into variants that mirror representative imagine-then-execute
WAM designs. For simplicity, we focus on single action chunk generation
and omit the outer auto-regressive loop. As shown in
Figure <a href="#S1.F1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>, existing WAMs can be broadly
grouped into two representative paradigms: *(A)* future videos and
actions are jointly denoised with shared
attention \[<a href="#bib.bib61" class="ltx_ref"
title="World action models are zero-shot policies">31</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib65" class="ltx_ref"
title="Unified world models: coupling video and action diffusion for pretraining on large robotic datasets">37</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib62" class="ltx_ref"
title="Motus: a unified latent action world model">2</a>\]; and *(B)*
actions are predicted after, and conditioned on, generated future
videos \[<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="Causal world modeling for robot control">17</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib64" class="ltx_ref"
title="Vidar: embodied video diffusion model for generalist manipulation">11</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib66" class="ltx_ref"
title="Learning universal policies via text-guided video generation">10</a>\].
We also implement a no-video-co-training variant, which serves as a
direct control for the role of the training objective itself. Together,
these controlled comparisons allow us to isolate the contribution of
test-time future imagination from that of video co-training during
training.

</div>

<div id="S1.p6" class="ltx_para">

Experiments on simulation benchmarks (LIBERO and RoboTwin) show that
Fast-WAM achieves strong results without any embodied pretraining,
demonstrating strong data efficiency. On real-world robotic tasks,
Fast-WAM remains highly effective while running at only 190 ms latency,
making it more than 4$`\times`$ faster than existing
imagine-then-execute WAM approaches. More importantly, controlled
comparisons show that Fast-WAM stays close to imagine-then-execute
variants, while removing the video co-training objective causes a much
larger performance drop. These results suggest that the main value of
video prediction in WAMs may lie in improving world representations
during training rather than in explicitly generating future observations
at test time.

</div>

<div id="S1.p7" class="ltx_para">

Our contributions are three-fold:

- <span id="S1.I1.i1"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span></span>
  <div id="S1.I1.i1.p1" class="ltx_para">

  We identify and study a basic question in WAMs: whether their gains
  come primarily from video modeling during training or from explicit
  future imagination during inference.

  </div>
- <span id="S1.I1.i2"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span></span>
  <div id="S1.I1.i2.p1" class="ltx_para">

  We propose Fast-WAM, a WAM architecture that retains video co-training
  during training while eliminating future prediction at test time,
  enabling real-time inference.

  </div>
- <span id="S1.I1.i3"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span></span>
  <div id="S1.I1.i3.p1" class="ltx_para">

  Through controlled comparisons on simulation and real-world
  benchmarks, including variants with and without video co-training, we
  show that much of the benefit of WAMs comes from the video co-training
  objective itself, while explicit future generation at inference time
  appears to be less critical than previously assumed.

  </div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S2" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">2 </span>Related Work

<div id="S2.SS0.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Vision-Language-Action Policies.

<div id="S2.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Recent progress in embodied foundation models has been driven by
Vision-Language-Action (VLA) policies, which directly map visual
observations and language instructions to robot actions using large
pretrained vision-language
backbones \[<a href="#bib.bib13" class="ltx_ref"
title="Openvla: an open-source vision-language-action model">16</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib14" class="ltx_ref"
title="π0: A vision-language-action flow model for general robot control">4</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib31" class="ltx_ref"
title="⁢pi0.5: A vision-language-action model with open-world generalization">13</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib24" class="ltx_ref"
title="Gr00t n1: an open foundation model for generalist humanoid robots">3</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib12" class="ltx_ref"
title="Rt-2: vision-language-action models transfer web knowledge to robotic control">38</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib17" class="ltx_ref"
title="Rdt-1b: a diffusion foundation model for bimanual manipulation">22</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib27" class="ltx_ref"
title="Agibot world colosseo: a large-scale manipulation platform for scalable and intelligent embodied systems">5</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib26" class="ltx_ref"
title="Smolvla: a vision-language-action model for affordable and efficient robotics">24</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib22" class="ltx_ref"
title="Gemini robotics: bringing ai into the physical world">26</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib32" class="ltx_ref"
title="Galaxea g0: open-world dataset and dual-system vla model">25</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib23" class="ltx_ref"
title="Dexvla: vision-language model with plug-in diffusion expert for general robot control">28</a>\].
By inheriting strong semantic priors from web-scale pretraining, these
models have shown strong generalization across objects, scenes, and
language instructions. However, as noted in recent WAM work, standard
VLA pretraining is largely based on static image-text data and does not
explicitly model how the physical world evolves under
action \[<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="Causal world modeling for robot control">17</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib61" class="ltx_ref"
title="World action models are zero-shot policies">31</a>\]. Our work is
complementary to this line: Fast-WAM preserves a direct-policy interface
at test time, similar to VLAs, but learns it under an additional
world-modeling objective based on future visual prediction.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S2.SS0.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### World Action Models and Video-based Robot Policies.

<div id="S2.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

A parallel line of work studies robot control through future visual
prediction, using video generation as a way to model environment
dynamics and infer actions \[<a href="#bib.bib66" class="ltx_ref"
title="Learning universal policies via text-guided video generation">10</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib81" class="ltx_ref"
title="Unleashing large-scale video generative pre-training for visual robot manipulation">30</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib78" class="ltx_ref"
title="RoboDreamer: learning compositional world models for robot imagination">36</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib64" class="ltx_ref"
title="Vidar: embodied video diffusion model for generalist manipulation">11</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib70" class="ltx_ref"
title="Gen2Act: human video generation in novel scenarios enables generalizable robot manipulation">1</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib73" class="ltx_ref"
title="Dual-stream diffusion for world-model augmented vision-language-action model">29</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib79" class="ltx_ref"
title="GR-2: a generative video-language-action model with web-scale knowledge for robot manipulation">8</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib80" class="ltx_ref"
title="DreamGen: unlocking generalization in robot learning through video world models">14</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib82" class="ltx_ref"
title="CoT-vla: visual chain-of-thought reasoning for vision-language-action models">33</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib83" class="ltx_ref"
title="RynnVLA-002: a unified vision-language-action and world model">6</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib84" class="ltx_ref"
title="WorldVLA: towards autoregressive action world model">7</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib86" class="ltx_ref"
title="Act2Goal: from world model to general goal-conditioned policy">35</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib85" class="ltx_ref"
title="FLARE: robot learning with implicit world modeling">34</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib87" class="ltx_ref"
title="DreamVLA: a vision-language-action model dreamed with comprehensive world knowledge">32</a>\].
Recent methods further scale this idea by jointly modeling future video
and robot actions in a unified
framework \[<a href="#bib.bib65" class="ltx_ref"
title="Unified world models: coupling video and action diffusion for pretraining on large robotic datasets">37</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib75" class="ltx_ref"
title="Video generators are robot policies">19</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib74" class="ltx_ref"
title="Cosmos policy: fine-tuning video models for visuomotor control and planning">15</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib76" class="ltx_ref"
title="Genie envisioner: a unified world foundation platform for robotic manipulation">20</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib77" class="ltx_ref"
title="Mimic-video: video-action models for generalizable robot control beyond vlas">23</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="Causal world modeling for robot control">17</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib62" class="ltx_ref"
title="Motus: a unified latent action world model">2</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib61" class="ltx_ref"
title="World action models are zero-shot policies">31</a>\]. We
follow \[<a href="#bib.bib61" class="ltx_ref"
title="World action models are zero-shot policies">31</a>\] to refer to
these models as World Action Models (WAMs), since they leverage world
modeling, i.e., predicting future visual states, to support downstream
action prediction. Most existing WAMs either follow an
imagine-then-execute paradigm, where future visual trajectories are
first generated and then used for action prediction, or jointly model
future video and actions within a shared generative process. Our work is
most closely related to this line, but differs in focus: rather than
proposing another imagine-then-execute WAM, we study whether the gains
of WAMs come primarily from video co-training during training or from
explicit future imagination during inference.

</div>

<div id="S2.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2" class="ltx_para">

Our work is also related to recent efforts that exploit video modeling
for action prediction while reducing or bypassing explicit test-time
video synthesis. VPP \[<a href="#bib.bib72" class="ltx_ref"
title="Video prediction policy: a generalist robot policy with predictive visual representations">12</a>\]
conditions robot policies on predictive visual representations extracted
from a video diffusion model, while
UVA \[<a href="#bib.bib71" class="ltx_ref"
title="Unified video action model">18</a>\] jointly models video and
action and skips video decoding at test time for faster inference.
Compared with these works, our focus is on disentangling the relative
roles of training-time video co-training and test-time future
imagination through controlled variants under a shared framework.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">3 </span>Method

<div id="S3.SS1" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.1 </span>Problem Formulation

<div id="S3.SS1.p1" class="ltx_para">

We consider embodied policy learning from visual observations and
language instructions. Let $`o`$ denote the current observation, $`l`$
denote the task instruction, and $`a_{1:H}`$ denote an action chunk of
horizon $`H`$. A standard visuomotor policy models the conditional
distribution

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
p(a_{1:H}\mid o,l),
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(1)</span> |

which directly maps the current perceptual context to a sequence of
actions. World Action Models (WAMs) augment this formulation by
introducing future visual observations as an intermediate variable. Let
$`v_{1:T}`$ denote future visual observations over a prediction horizon
$`T`$. Many existing WAMs follow an *imagine-then-execute*
factorization:

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
p(a_{1:H}\mid o,l)=\int p(v_{1:T}\mid o,l)\,p(a_{1:H}\mid o,l,v_{1:T})\,dv_{1:T},
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(2)</span> |

where the model first predicts future observations and then conditions
action generation on the imagined future. In practice, this is typically
implemented either by jointly denoising future video and actions within
a shared model, or by first generating future video and then feeding it
to an inverse dynamics or action prediction module. Some prior WAMs
further wrap this formulation in an outer auto-regressive rollout, which
we omit here for simplicity and controlled comparison.

</div>

<div id="S3.SS1.p2" class="ltx_para">

Our starting point is the observation that the effectiveness of WAMs may
arise from two distinct factors: *(i)* the video prediction objective
used during training, which can encourage the model to learn physically
meaningful latent representations, and *(ii)* explicit future generation
during inference, which may provide additional foresight for action
prediction. Existing WAM formulations usually couple these two factors,
since the same model both learns from future video prediction and
explicitly synthesizes future observations at test time. We design
Fast-WAM to decouple these two factors. During training, it retains
world modeling as a co-training signal; during inference, however, it
does not explicitly generate future observations. Instead, Fast-WAM
predicts actions directly from the current observation and instruction,

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
p_{\theta}(a_{1:H}\mid o,l),
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(3)</span> |

while using latent world representations shaped by video co-training. In
this sense, Fast-WAM has a direct-policy interface at test time, similar
to standard VLA policies, while its representation learning remains
grounded in WAM-style video modeling during training. Formally, let
$`z(o,l)`$ denote the latent world representation produced by the video
backbone conditioned on the current context. Fast-WAM uses this
representation to parameterize the action distribution,

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
p_{\theta}(a_{1:H}\mid o,l)=p_{\theta}(a_{1:H}\mid z(o,l)).
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(4)</span> |

The key difference from imagine-then-execute WAMs is that $`z(o,l)`$ is
obtained by a single forward encoding pass, rather than by explicitly
sampling or denoising future observations $`v_{1:T}`$ at inference time.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS2" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.2 </span>Model Architecture

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Overview.

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Fast-WAM is designed to preserve the training benefits of world modeling
while removing the inference cost of explicit future imagination. During
training, it jointly learns action prediction and video modeling,
encouraging the visual backbone to capture physically meaningful motion
and interaction structure. During inference, Fast-WAM does not
explicitly generate future observations. Instead, it keeps only the
clean latent tokens of the first observation frame, processes them with
the video model in a single forward pass, and uses the resulting latent
world representation for direct action generation. This gives Fast-WAM a
direct-policy interface at test time while retaining WAM-style video
supervision during training.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Architecture.

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

Fast-WAM is built on top of the video Diffusion Transformer (DiT) from
Wan2.2-5B \[<a href="#bib.bib67" class="ltx_ref"
title="Wan: open and advanced large-scale video generative models">27</a>\],
which serves as the world modeling backbone. We also reuse its
pretrained text encoder and video VAE: task language is encoded by the
built-in T5 encoder and provided to all tokens through cross-attention,
while visual observations are mapped into latent video tokens by the
pretrained VAE. On top of this backbone, we introduce an action expert
DiT for action chunk generation. The full model is organized as a
Mixture-of-Transformer (MoT) architecture with shared attention between
the video and action branches, as illustrated in
Figure <a href="#S3.F2.sf1" class="ltx_ref"
title="In Figure 2 ‣ Architecture. ‣ 3.2 Model Architecture ‣ 3 Method ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2(a)</span></a>.

</div>

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p2" class="ltx_para">

We organize the input tokens into three groups: clean latent tokens of
the first observation frame, which serve as the shared visual anchor;
noisy latent tokens of future video frames, which are used only during
training for video modeling; and action tokens, which are processed by
the action expert for action generation. All token groups attend to the
language embeddings through cross-attention. A structured attention mask
controls the information flow between these groups. During training,
future noisy video tokens attend bidirectionally within the video branch
and can access the clean first-frame tokens; action tokens attend
bidirectionally within the action branch and can also access the clean
first-frame tokens. Crucially, action tokens cannot attend to future
video tokens, and the clean first-frame tokens do not attend to any
other tokens. This ensures that both video modeling and action
prediction are grounded in the same visual context while preventing
future information from leaking into the action branch. We provide the
full training and inference masks in
Figure <a href="#S3.F2.sf2" class="ltx_ref"
title="In Figure 2 ‣ Architecture. ‣ 3.2 Model Architecture ‣ 3 Method ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2(b)</span></a>.

</div>

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p3" class="ltx_para">

At inference time, Fast-WAM removes the future video branch entirely:
only the clean first-frame latent tokens are retained and passed through
the video backbone once to produce latent world features for the action
expert. Since no future noisy video tokens are instantiated and no
explicit future video denoising is performed, Fast-WAM incurs
substantially lower inference cost than standard imagine-then-execute
WAMs.

</div>

<figure id="S3.F2" class="ltx_figure">
<div class="ltx_flex_figure">
<div class="ltx_flex_cell ltx_flex_size_2">
<figure id="S3.F2.sf1"
class="ltx_figure ltx_figure_panel ltx_align_center">
<img src="2603.16666v2/x2.png" id="S3.F2.sf1.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="830"
height="605" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span
id="S3.F2.sf1.2.1.1" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">(a)</span>
</span><span id="S3.F2.sf1.3.2" class="ltx_text"
style="font-size:90%;">Fast-WAM model architecture.</span></figcaption>
</figure>
</div>
<div class="ltx_flex_cell ltx_flex_size_2">
<figure id="S3.F2.sf2"
class="ltx_figure ltx_figure_panel ltx_align_center">
<img src="2603.16666v2/x3.png" id="S3.F2.sf2.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_portrait" width="828"
height="1807" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span
id="S3.F2.sf2.2.1.1" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">(b)</span>
</span><span id="S3.F2.sf2.3.2" class="ltx_text"
style="font-size:90%;">Training and inference masks.</span></figcaption>
</figure>
</div>
</div>
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="S3.F2.2.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 2</span>: </span><span
id="S3.F2.3.2" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Fast-WAM
architecture and the structured attention mask used to disentangle video
co-training from action generation.</span></figcaption>
</figure>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px3" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Training objective.

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1" class="ltx_para">

Fast-WAM is trained with a joint flow matching objective over action
tokens and future video latents. Given a target variable $`y`$ (either
an action chunk $`a_{1:H}`$ or future video latents $`z_{1:T}`$), we
sample Gaussian noise $`\epsilon\sim\mathcal{N}(0,I)`$ and a time step
$`t\in(0,1)`$, and construct the interpolated sample

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
y_{t}=(1-t)\,y+t\,\epsilon.
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(5)</span> |

The model is trained to predict the corresponding velocity field with a
standard flow matching objective

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{FM}}(y)=\mathbb{E}_{y,\,\epsilon,\,t}\left[\left\|f_{\theta}(y_{t},\,t,\,o,\,l)-(\epsilon-y)\right\|_{2}^{2}\right].
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(6)</span> |

</div>

<div id="S3.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p2" class="ltx_para">

We instantiate this objective for both action generation and video
co-training. For action prediction, we set $`y=a_{1:H}`$ and optimize

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{act}}=\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{FM}}(a_{1:H}).
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(7)</span> |

For video co-training, we set $`y=z_{1:T}`$, where $`z_{1:T}`$ denotes
the latent tokens of future video frames produced by the pretrained VAE,
and optimize

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{vid}}=\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{FM}}(z_{1:T}).
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(8)</span> |

The overall training objective is

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\mathcal{L}=\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{act}}+\lambda\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{vid}},
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(9)</span> |

where $`\lambda`$ balances action learning and video co-training.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS3" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.3 </span>Controlled Variants for Disentangled WAM Design

<div id="S3.SS3.p1" class="ltx_para">

To answer our central question, namely whether the benefit of WAMs comes
primarily from video co-training during training or from explicit future
imagination during inference, we design a set of controlled variants
under a shared implementation framework. We instantiate representative
imagine-then-execute design patterns from recent WAMs while keeping the
backbone, tokenization, and training recipe as aligned as possible. This
controlled setup allows us to isolate the contribution of test-time
future generation from that of the video co-training objective itself.

</div>

<div id="S3.SS3.p2" class="ltx_para">

As illustrated in Figure <a href="#S1.F1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>(A) and (B), we consider two
representative imagine-then-execute variants that capture the dominant
design patterns in recent WAMs. The first variant, named Fast-WAM-Joint,
follows the joint-generation paradigm, where future video tokens and
action tokens are denoised together within a shared model, so that
action generation remains coupled with future video modeling throughout
the denoising process \[<a href="#bib.bib61" class="ltx_ref"
title="World action models are zero-shot policies">31</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib65" class="ltx_ref"
title="Unified world models: coupling video and action diffusion for pretraining on large robotic datasets">37</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib62" class="ltx_ref"
title="Motus: a unified latent action world model">2</a>\]. The second
variant, named Fast-WAM-IDM, follows the video-then-action paradigm,
where future video tokens are generated first from the current
observation and language context, and action prediction is then
conditioned on the resulting future
representation \[<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="Causal world modeling for robot control">17</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib64" class="ltx_ref"
title="Vidar: embodied video diffusion model for generalist manipulation">11</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib66" class="ltx_ref"
title="Learning universal policies via text-guided video generation">10</a>\].
In both cases, we preserve the defining inference structure of the
corresponding paradigm, together with key training choices used in
recent WAMs, while implementing them within our shared framework for
controlled comparison with Fast-WAM.

</div>

<div id="S3.SS3.p3" class="ltx_para">

We further construct a Fast-WAM variant without video co-training. This
variant keeps the architecture and inference procedure unchanged, and
removes only the video modeling objective during training. It therefore
serves as a direct control for the role of video co-training itself.
Together, these controlled variants isolate the two factors that are
typically entangled in prior WAMs: video co-training during training and
explicit future imagination during inference.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S4" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">4 </span>Experiment

<div id="S4.SS1" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.1 </span>Implementation Details

<div id="S4.SS1.p1" class="ltx_para">

We use pretrained Wan2.2-5B \[<a href="#bib.bib67" class="ltx_ref"
title="Wan: open and advanced large-scale video generative models">27</a>\]
as the backbone, including its video DiT, text encoder, and video VAE.
The action expert shares the same architecture as the video branch but
uses a reduced hidden dimension of $`d_{a}=1024`$, resulting in a 1B
action expert and a total model size of 6B parameters. We set the action
horizon to $`h=32`$. Video frames are temporally downsampled by
$`4\times`$, resulting in 9 video frames per chunk. Images from multiple
cameras are concatenated into a single image before being fed into the
VAE.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS1.p2" class="ltx_para">

We use the same flow-matching formulation for both the video and action
branches. Following \[<a href="#bib.bib67" class="ltx_ref"
title="Wan: open and advanced large-scale video generative models">27</a>\],
we adopt a logit-normal distribution over $`t`$ as the noise schedule
during both training and inference. During inference, we use 10
denoising steps with classifier-free guidance (CFG) scale set to 1.0. We
use AdamW with a learning rate of $`1\times 10^{-4}`$, weight decay of
0.01, and a cosine annealing schedule for all training settings.
Training is conducted in mixed precision with gradient clipping at 1.0.
All latency numbers are measured on a single NVIDIA RTX 5090D V2 32GB
GPU.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS1.p3" class="ltx_para">

We build Fast-WAM-Joint by allowing attention between video and action
tokens. For Fast-WAM-IDM, we
follow \[<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="Causal world modeling for robot control">17</a>\] and apply noise
augmentation to the ground-truth video tokens with probability
$`p=0.5`$.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S4.SS2" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.2 </span>Experiment Setup

<div id="S4.SS2.p1" class="ltx_para">

We evaluate Fast-WAM and its variants on both simulation benchmarks,
namely LIBERO \[<a href="#bib.bib68" class="ltx_ref"
title="LIBERO: benchmarking knowledge transfer for lifelong robot learning">21</a>\]
and RoboTwin 2.0 \[<a href="#bib.bib69" class="ltx_ref"
title="Robotwin 2.0: a scalable data generator and benchmark with strong domain randomization for robust bimanual robotic manipulation">9</a>\],
as well as on a real-world manipulation task.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### LIBERO.

<div id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

We follow the standard benchmarking protocol and train our models on the
four LIBERO suites: LIBERO-Spatial, LIBERO-Object, LIBERO-Goal, and
LIBERO-Long. Each suite contains 500 demonstrations spanning 10 tasks.
All models are trained for 20k steps. We report success rates for each
suite, evaluated over a total of 2000 trials across 40 tasks with
different random seeds.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### RoboTwin 2.0.

<div id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

RoboTwin 2.0 is a challenging bimanual manipulation benchmark featuring
more than 50 tasks that require coordinated dual-arm control. We follow
the multi-task training setup of \[<a href="#bib.bib62" class="ltx_ref"
title="Motus: a unified latent action world model">2</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="Causal world modeling for robot control">17</a>\], where models
are trained on a mixture of 2,500 demonstrations collected in clean
scenes and 25,000 demonstrations collected under heavy scene
randomization, spanning over 50 tasks. All models are trained for 30k
steps. We report average success rates over 100 trials per task under
both clean and randomized settings.

</div>

<figure id="S4.F3" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="2603.16666v2/figs/bench_grid_2560x720.png" id="S4.F3.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="476"
height="134" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="S4.F3.2.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 3</span>: </span><span
id="S4.F3.3.2" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Real-world
towel-folding task on the Galaxea R1 Lite platform. Folding a deformable
object requires long-horizon planning and precise closed-loop
manipulation, making it a challenging benchmark for evaluating both task
success and execution efficiency.</span></figcaption>
</figure>

</div>

<div id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px3" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Real-World Evaluation.

<div id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1" class="ltx_para">

We conduct real-world evaluation on towel folding, a long-horizon and
challenging task that requires the policy to reason about the dynamics
of deformable objects, as shown in
Figure <a href="#S4.F3" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 3 ‣ RoboTwin 2.0. ‣ 4.2 Experiment Setup ‣ 4 Experiment ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>. We collect 60 hours of
teleoperated demonstrations on the Galaxea R1 Lite platform. All models
are trained for 30k steps. We report both the average success rate and
the average completion time. The former measures whether the policy can
eventually complete the folding task given sufficient time, while the
latter reflects whether the policy learns an efficient execution
strategy rather than relying on repeated trial-and-error corrections.
For this task, completion time is therefore as important as success rate
in assessing policy quality.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.3 </span>Main Results

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS1" class="section ltx_subsubsection">

<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 1: </span>Results
on RoboTwin. Fast-WAM matches strong pretrained WAM baselines without
using embodied pretraining, while the two imagine-then-execute variants
remain highly comparable and removing video co-training causes a
substantial drop.</figcaption>
</figure>

<td id="S4.T2.2.2.9.5"

</div>
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 2: </span>Results
on LIBERO. Fast-WAM achieves competitive overall performance without
embodied pretraining, remains close to both imagine-then-execute
variants, and outperforms the no-video-co-training ablation by a clear
margin.</figcaption>
</figure>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Table <a href="#S4.T1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 1 ‣ 4.3.1 Overall comparison on simulation benchmarks ‣ 4.3 Main Results ‣ 4 Experiment ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a> and
Table <a href="#S4.T2" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 2 ‣ 4.3.1 Overall comparison on simulation benchmarks ‣ 4.3 Main Results ‣ 4 Experiment ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a> summarize the results on
RoboTwin and LIBERO, respectively. Overall, Fast-WAM achieves
performance comparable to state-of-the-art methods on both benchmarks
without using embodied pretraining. Per-task results on RoboTwin are
listed in Appendix Table <a href="#A1.T3" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 3 ‣ A.1 RoboTwin Detailed Results ‣ Appendix A Appendix ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS1.p2" class="ltx_para">

On RoboTwin, Fast-WAM achieves 91.8% success rate, exceeding all
baselines without embodied pretraining and remaining highly competitive
with the strongest pretrained WAMs. In particular, Fast-WAM
substantially outperforms Motus \[<a href="#bib.bib62" class="ltx_ref"
title="Motus: a unified latent action world model">2</a>\] both with
pretraining (87.8%) and without pretraining (77.3%), as well as
LingBot-VA \[<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="Causal world modeling for robot control">17</a>\] without
pretraining (80.6%), while remaining comparable to pretrained LingBot-VA
(92.2%). These results suggest that Fast-WAM can recover most of the
benefit of prior WAM pipelines without relying on embodied pretraining.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS1.p3" class="ltx_para">

On LIBERO, Fast-WAM also delivers strong overall performance, achieving
an average success rate of 97.6% without embodied pretraining. It
outperforms the strong VLA baseline $`\pi_{0.5}`$ and remains
competitive with pretrained WAM baselines such as LingBot-VA (98.5%) and
Motus (97.7%). These results show that Fast-WAM is consistently
effective across diverse simulation benchmarks, even without the
embodied pretraining used by most prior WAM and VLA baselines.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS2" class="section ltx_subsubsection">

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">4.3.2 </span>Controlled comparison with Fast-WAM variants

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS2.p1" class="ltx_para">

We next compare Fast-WAM with the controlled variants introduced in
Sec. <a href="#S3.SS3" class="ltx_ref"
title="3.3 Controlled Variants for Disentangled WAM Design ‣ 3 Method ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3.3</span></a> to disentangle the role of
explicit future imagination at inference time from that of video
co-training during training. Across both simulation benchmarks, the
overall pattern is consistent: Fast-WAM remains comparable to the two
imagine-then-execute variants, while removing video co-training leads to
a much larger performance drop.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS2.p2" class="ltx_para">

On RoboTwin, Fast-WAM achieves 91.8% success rate, which is highly
comparable to both Fast-WAM-Joint (90.6%) and Fast-WAM-IDM (91.3%). In
contrast, removing video co-training reduces performance to 83.8%,
yielding a substantial gap relative to all three variants with video
co-training. This result suggests that, on this benchmark, retaining the
video modeling objective during training is far more important than
explicitly generating future observations at test time. A similar trend
holds on LIBERO. Fast-WAM reaches 97.6% average success rate, remaining
close to Fast-WAM-Joint (98.5%) and Fast-WAM-IDM (98.0%). By comparison,
Fast-WAM without video co-training drops to 93.5%, with especially
visible degradation on the <span id="S4.SS3.SSS2.p2.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">Spatial</span> and
<span id="S4.SS3.SSS2.p2.1.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">Long</span> subsets. Again, the gap
caused by removing video co-training is clearly larger than the gap
between Fast-WAM and the imagine-then-execute variants.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS2.p3" class="ltx_para">

This pattern suggests that the main benefit of WAM-style training may
lie less in whether, or how, future imagination is performed at test
time, and more in the video prediction objective used to shape
world-grounded representations during training.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS3" class="section ltx_subsubsection">

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">4.3.3 </span>Real-world performance and efficiency

<figure id="S4.F4" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="2603.16666v2/x4.png" id="S4.F4.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="627"
height="255" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="S4.F4.2.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 4</span>: </span><span
id="S4.F4.3.2" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Real-world
results on the long-horizon towel-folding task. The left panel plots
success rate against average completion time, where upper-left is
better. The right panel compares inference latency. Fast-WAM achieves
strong real-world performance with substantially lower latency than
imagine-then-execute variants, while removing video co-training degrades
both success rate and completion time.</span></figcaption>
</figure>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS3.p1" class="ltx_para">

We further evaluate Fast-WAM on a long-horizon real-world towel-folding
task. We report both the average success rate and the average completion
time, as they capture two equally important aspects of policy quality:
whether the policy can eventually complete the task, and whether it does
so efficiently rather than through repeated trial-and-error corrections.
Results are shown in Figure <a href="#S4.F4" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 4 ‣ 4.3.3 Real-world performance and efficiency ‣ 4.3 Main Results ‣ 4 Experiment ‣ Fast-WAM: Do World Action Models Need Test-time Future Imagination?"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS3.p2" class="ltx_para">

On this task, pretrained $`\pi_{0.5}`$ remains the strongest method,
achieving the highest success rate together with the shortest completion
time. Among the Fast-WAM family, the overall performance is comparable,
with Fast-WAM-IDM achieving the highest success rate, while Fast-WAM
attains a better completion time. Importantly, all Fast-WAM variants
with video co-training substantially outperform $`\pi_{0.5}`$ without
pretraining, indicating that WAM-style video co-training provides strong
data efficiency even without embodied pretraining.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS3.p3" class="ltx_para">

In contrast, removing video co-training causes a dramatic degradation on
both metrics: Fast-WAM without video co-training drops to only 10%
success rate and also requires the longest completion time among all
methods. This gap is much larger than the differences among the Fast-WAM
variants themselves, suggesting that video co-training is the dominant
factor behind strong real-world performance, whereas the effect of
test-time future imagination is comparatively limited.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.SSS3.p4" class="ltx_para">

In terms of runtime, Fast-WAM retains low inference latency (190 ms),
whereas the imagine-then-execute variants are substantially slower,
especially Fast-WAM-IDM at 810 ms. This makes Fast-WAM a more favorable
design point for real-world deployment, offering strong task performance
together with much lower inference cost.

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S5" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">5 </span>Conclusion

<div id="S5.p1" class="ltx_para">

In this paper, we revisited a basic question in World Action Models:
whether their gains come primarily from explicit future imagination at
test time or from video modeling during training. To study this
question, we introduced Fast-WAM, a WAM architecture that retains video
co-training during training while skipping future prediction at
inference time, enabling direct action generation from world-grounded
latent representations. Across simulation benchmarks and real-world
robotic tasks, Fast-WAM achieves strong performance without embodied
pretraining while running in real time. More importantly, controlled
comparisons show that Fast-WAM remains competitive with
imagine-then-execute variants, whereas removing video co-training leads
to a much larger degradation. These results suggest that the main value
of video prediction in WAMs may lie more in learning better world
representations during training than in generating future observations
at test time. An important direction for future work is to study the
effect of larger-scale pretraining data and model scaling on this
design.

</div>

<div class="ltx_pagination ltx_role_newpage">

</div>

</div>

<div id="A1" class="section ltx_appendix">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_appendix">Appendix A </span>Appendix

<div id="A1.SS1" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">A.1 </span>RoboTwin Detailed Results

<div id="A1.SS1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Here we present the per-task results on RoboTwin evaluation in
Table <a href="#A1.T3" class="ltx_ref"

## Instructions for reporting errors

We are continuing to improve HTML versions of papers, and your feedback
helps enhance accessibility and mobile support. To report errors in the
HTML that will help us improve conversion and rendering, choose any of
the methods listed below:

- Click the "Report Issue"
  <span class="mobile-only">(![](data:image/svg+xml;base64,PHN2ZyByb2xlPSJwcmVzZW50YXRpb24iIHN0eWxlPSJkaXNwbGF5OiBpbmxpbmUtYmxvY2s7IHZlcnRpY2FsLWFsaWduOiBtaWRkbGU7IGZpbGw6IHZhcigtLXRleHQtY29sb3IpOyIgYXJpYS1oaWRkZW49InRydWUiIGhlaWdodD0iMWVtIiB2aWV3Ym94PSIwIDAgNjQwIDY0MCI+CiAgICAgICAgICAgIDxwYXRoIGQ9Ik0yMjQgMTYwQzIyNCAxMDcgMjY3IDY0IDMyMCA2NEMzNzMgNjQgNDE2IDEwNyA0MTYgMTYwTDQxNiAxNjMuNkM0MTYgMTc5LjMgNDAzLjMgMTkyIDM4Ny42IDE5MkwyNTIuNSAxOTJDMjM2LjggMTkyIDIyNC4xIDE3OS4zIDIyNC4xIDE2My42TDIyNC4xIDE2MHpNNTY5LjYgMTcyLjhDNTgwLjIgMTg2LjkgNTc3LjMgMjA3IDU2My4yIDIxNy42TDQ2NS40IDI5MC45QzQ3MC43IDI5OS44IDQ3NC43IDMwOS42IDQ3Ny4yIDMyMEw1NzYgMzIwQzU5My43IDMyMCA2MDggMzM0LjMgNjA4IDM1MkM2MDggMzY5LjcgNTkzLjcgMzg0IDU3NiAzODRMNDgwIDM4NEw0ODAgNDE2QzQ4MCA0MTguNiA0NzkuOSA0MjEuMyA0NzkuOCA0MjMuOUw1NjMuMiA0ODYuNEM1NzcuMyA0OTcgNTgwLjIgNTE3LjEgNTY5LjYgNTMxLjJDNTU5IDU0NS4zIDUzOC45IDU0OC4yIDUyNC44IDUzNy42TDQ2MS43IDQ5MC4zQzQzOC41IDUzNC41IDM5NS4yIDU2Ni41IDM0NCA1NzQuMkwzNDQgMzQ0QzM0NCAzMzAuNyAzMzMuMyAzMjAgMzIwIDMyMEMzMDYuNyAzMjAgMjk2IDMzMC43IDI5NiAzNDRMMjk2IDU3NC4yQzI0NC44IDU2Ni41IDIwMS41IDUzNC41IDE3OC4zIDQ5MC4zTDExNS4yIDUzNy42QzEwMS4xIDU0OC4yIDgxIDU0NS4zIDcwLjQgNTMxLjJDNTkuOCA1MTcuMSA2Mi43IDQ5NyA3Ni44IDQ4Ni40TDE2MC4yIDQyMy45QzE2MC4xIDQyMS4zIDE2MCA0MTguNyAxNjAgNDE2TDE2MCAzODRMNjQgMzg0QzQ2LjMgMzg0IDMyIDM2OS43IDMyIDM1MkMzMiAzMzQuMyA0Ni4zIDMyMCA2NCAzMjBMMTYyLjggMzIwQzE2NS4zIDMwOS42IDE2OS4zIDI5OS44IDE3NC42IDI5MC45TDc2LjggMjE3LjZDNjIuNyAyMDcgNTkuOCAxODYuOSA3MC40IDE3Mi44QzgxIDE1OC43IDEwMS4xIDE1NS44IDExNS4yIDE2Ni40TDIyNCAyNDhDMjM2LjMgMjQyLjkgMjQ5LjggMjQwIDI2NCAyNDBMMzc2IDI0MEMzOTAuMiAyNDAgNDAzLjcgMjQyLjggNDE2IDI0OEw1MjQuOCAxNjYuNEM1MzguOSAxNTUuOCA1NTkgMTU4LjcgNTY5LjYgMTcyLjh6IiAvPgogICAgICAgICAgPC9zdmc+))</span>
  button, located in the page header.

**Tip:** You can select the relevant text first, to include it in your
report.

Our team has already identified
<a href="https://github.com/arXiv/html_feedback/issues" class="ltx_ref"
target="_blank">the following issues</a>. We appreciate your time
reviewing and reporting rendering errors we may not have found yet. Your
efforts will help us improve the HTML versions for all readers, because
disability should not be a barrier to accessing research. Thank you for
your continued support in championing open access for all.

Have a free development cycle? Help support accessibility at arXiv! Our
collaborators at LaTeXML maintain a <a
href="https://github.com/brucemiller/LaTeXML/wiki/Porting-LaTeX-packages-for-LaTeXML"
class="ltx_ref" target="_blank">list of packages that need
conversion</a>, and welcome
<a href="https://github.com/brucemiller/LaTeXML/issues" class="ltx_ref"
target="_blank">developer contributions</a>.
